<div id="review_1114664357" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1114664357" />
    <a title="Russell Bittner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2270932-russell-bittner"><img alt="Russell Bittner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442501525p2/2270932.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1114664357?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Russell Bittner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Russell Bittner" href="/user/show/2270932-russell-bittner">Russell Bittner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1114664357" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer710693270123716430">I must confess, I picked this novel up only because I’d recently read that the wife of Paul Bowles (a rather well-regarded twentieth-century itinerant writer and composer) was the author and was, herself, a woman of much talent but limited repute. I believe I actually saw her described as “a writer’s writer.”<br/><br/>If so, I guess I ain’t no writer – or, at the very least, I can’t support that particular view of Jane Bowles’s work.<br/><br/><i>
  <b>Two Serious Ladies</b>
</i> is, in a nutshell, bizarre – and I don’t mean beca</span>
  <span id="freeText710693270123716430" style="display:none">I must confess, I picked this novel up only because I’d recently read that the wife of Paul Bowles (a rather well-regarded twentieth-century itinerant writer and composer) was the author and was, herself, a woman of much talent but limited repute. I believe I actually saw her described as “a writer’s writer.”<br /><br />If so, I guess I ain’t no writer – or, at the very least, I can’t support that particular view of Jane Bowles’s work.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>Two Serious Ladies</b>
</i> is, in a nutshell, bizarre – and I don’t mean because of its content. I mean that the writing is bizarre. On the one hand, I kept asking myself whether English was really Ms. Bowles’s native language. On the other hand, the descriptors ‘fey’ and ‘airy-fairy’ occurred to me over and over again. I was consequently not in the least surprised that Tennessee Williams should’ve proclaimed <i>
  <b>Two Serious Ladies</b>
</i> “(m)y favorite book” – and added – “I can’t think of a modern novel that seems more likely to become a classic.”<br /><br />I’m sorry. I really wanted to like it – and to be able to declare with Claire Messud, who wrote the Introduction, that “I (too) simply could not put it down.” My problem was the opposite: I kept having to poke myself to pick the book back up and read more of Ms. Bowles’s drivel.<br /><br />Yet I plunged on, wanting to find out why: “John Ashbury called Jane Bowles ‘one of the finest modern writers of fiction in any language’; Alan Sillitoe anointed the novel ‘a landmark in twentieth-century American literature’; Truman Capote deemed her ‘one of the really original pure stylists’; James Purdy said she was ‘an unmatchable talent’; and Tennessee Williams (once again) announced that she was ‘the most important writer of prose fiction in modern American letters’” – all on p. vi of Claire Messud’s Introduction.<br /><br />I had to wonder whether Ms. Bowles had been trading sexual favors for flattering reviews – or, more likely (given their separate but equal sexual proclivities), maybe this was payback time to <i>Paul</i> Bowles for a bit of past authorial kink.<br /><br />To take just a random example (this one on p. 72): “‘All right,’ said Mr. Copperfield. He looked sad and lonely. He enjoyed so much showing other people the things he liked best. He started to walk away towards the edge of the water and stared out across the river at the opposite shore. He was very slight and his head was beautifully shaped.”<br /><br />Why would a man who’d been married to the same woman for decades suddenly look “sad and lonely” because she opted not to accompany him on a little stroll through the Panamanian jungle? Disappointed, yes. Annoyed, yes. Possibly nonplused if he’s like most men whose wives change their minds at the last minute. But “sad and lonely?” <i>Really?</i> And would that same woman then suddenly observe that that same husband of ten thousand and one nights between the sheets now appeared to her to be “very slight(,) and his head was beautifully shaped?” If he’d been reaching up for a banana in that same instant (not out of place, given the setting of the incident), she might well have observed that he was ‘a simian delight to behold, my exuberant little tropical punch,’ but God knows <i>not</i> that “(h)e was slight(,) and his head was beautifully shaped.”<br /><br />(Please forgive: I first learned the word ‘simian’ forty years ago <i>chez</i> Theodore Dreiser –who in fact used it three times in the same novel – and I’ve been dying to use it myself ever since!)<br /><br />Or maybe this is the answer (on p. 76), ostensibly from the mouth (or thoughts – it’s always a little difficult to tell with Ms. Bowles’s idiosyncratic punctuation) of Mrs. Copperfield, although I think we can safely assume that that same Mrs. Copperfield serves as something of a mouthpiece for Ms. Bowles here and elsewhere: “‘Now,’ she said, jumping off the bed, ‘now for a little spot of gin to chase my troubles away. There just isn’t any other way that’s as good. At a certain point(,) gin takes everything off your hands(,) and you flop around like a little baby. Tonight(,) I want to be a little baby.’”<br /><br />I like a snifterful (or “hookerful,” as she calls it in the sentence immediately following) as much as the next guy or chick, but I’m also ever-mindful of Hemingway’s dictum: “Write drunk; edit sober.” I have to wonder whether Ms. Bowles ever bothered to pull herself up from under the table long enough to heed the second part of that dictum.<br /><br />	I <i>will</i> give Ms. Bowles credit for one rather trenchant observation early on in the novel – viz., “(t)ourists, generally speaking,” Mrs. Copperfield had written in her journal, “are human beings so impressed with the importance and immutability of their own manner of living that they are capable of traveling through the most fantastic places without experiencing anything more than a visual reaction. The hardier tourists find that one place resembles another.”<br /><br />	As she and her husband were particularly well-traveled, I have to concede to her a well-earned authority in this quasi-aphorism. I just don’t understand how it could’ve been penned by the same hand that wrote so much tripe. Maybe – just <i>maybe</i> – she was actually sober when she wrote it.<br /><br /><br />	But the long and short of it is that this book, in my opinion, is an amateur piece of work – <b>AMATEUR</b> writ large and bold. There is one anecdote or action after another that leads nowhere and hardly advances the plot of the book – if advancing the plot was ever even a thought in Jane Bowles’s head. Categorize it however you like – modern; post-modern; post-post-modern; irony; parody; buffoonery; critical social commentary – it just didn’t work, at least for <i>this</i> particular reader.<br /><br /><br />	But as I never fail to add, <i>de gustibus non est disputandum.</i> If my fellow reviewers found the work enchanting, I’m certainly in no position to question their judgment or their choice of enchantment.<br /><br /><b>RRB<br />11/30/14<br />Brooklyn, NY</b><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="710693270123716430" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1513621115" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1513621115" />
    <a title="Claire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2528841-claire"><img alt="Claire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336501182p2/2528841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1513621115?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claire" href="/user/show/2528841-claire">Claire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780956003850" href="/book/show/7662153-two-serious-ladies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=500-great-books-by-women">500-great-books-by-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=classic">classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1513621115" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14129658419385619031">Two Serious Ladies introduces us to two characters Christina Goering, daughter of a powerful industrialist, now a well-heeled spinster, adrift and bored with her comfortable, predictable existence and Frieda Copperfield, married to a man who pursues travel and adventure, dragging his wife (who funds this insatiable desire) out of her comfort zone, to the untouristed, red-lit parts of Panama, where she finds solace and digs her heels in, at the bar/hotel of Madame Quill, befriending the young pro</span>
  <span id="freeText14129658419385619031" style="display:none">Two Serious Ladies introduces us to two characters Christina Goering, daughter of a powerful industrialist, now a well-heeled spinster, adrift and bored with her comfortable, predictable existence and Frieda Copperfield, married to a man who pursues travel and adventure, dragging his wife (who funds this insatiable desire) out of her comfort zone, to the untouristed, red-lit parts of Panama, where she finds solace and digs her heels in, at the bar/hotel of Madame Quill, befriending the young prostitute Pacifica.<br /><br />Christina, referred to as Miss Goering and Frieda, Mrs Copperfield, acquaintances, meet briefly at a party and will come together again briefly at the end, both having had separate life-changing adventures, driven by a latent, sub-conscious desire to radically change their situations, both of which come about in a random, haphazard way.<br /><br />Miss Goering invites a companion Miss Gamelon, to move into her comfortable home and at the party where she encounters Mrs Copperfield, she meets Arnold. Though she doesn’t particularly like either of these characters, when she decides to sell her palatial home and move to a run-down house on a nearby island, they agree to come with her. Neither are enamoured of her decision, to remove them from her previous comforts, which they were quite enjoying.<br /><br />“In my opinion,” said Miss Gamelon, “you could perfectly well work out your salvation during certain hours of the day without having to move everything.”<br /><br /><blockquote>“The idea,” said Miss Goering, “is to change first of our own volition and according to our own inner promptings before they impose completely arbitrary changes on us.”</blockquote><br /><br />Once on the island, still restless, she abandons her invitees and takes the ferry to the mainland, opening herself up to whatever random encounters await her, as if seeking her destiny or some kind of understanding through a series of desperate and reckless acts.<br /><br />Mrs Copperfield seems less to seek out the depraved, than be attracted by a perceived sense of belonging, she spurns the comfortable, pretentious trappings of the Hotel Washington, declines to go walking in the jungle with her husband and instead takes the bus back to the women she has met at the Hotel les Palmas whom she feels an affinity with, despite their lives of poverty and prostitution being so far removed from her own. She recognises they possess a kind of freedom and strength she lacks; in their presence, she begins to feel energised and empowered.<br /><br />It is a strange book at first, it requires finishing and reflecting upon to figure out what it was all about. It is recounted in a straight forward style, we observe the actions of the two women without reflection on their part, making it necessary to unravel their intentions, which inevitably becomes a matter of reader interpretation, to find the meaning, if indeed there is any.<br /><br />For me, it was clear the women lacked something significant in their lives, in their existence, even if they were unable to articulate it or even search appropriately for it, they sensed something missing in their lives of privilege and sought it among the downtrodden. They were experiencing an existential crisis.<br /><br />Bowles takes two female characters from a similar social class (similar to her own) dissecting a woman’s presence and existence in society in a form of confrontational daring that was liable to elicit both scorn and eye-brow raising in her own time and continues to provoke a certain amount of bemusement in our own.<br /><br /><blockquote>“I know I am as guilty as I can be, but I have my happiness which I guard like a wolf, and I have authority now and a certain amount of daring, which I never had before.” – Mrs Copperfield</blockquote><br /><br />Reading it alongside the life of Jane Bowles, was a pleasure, I enjoyed reading it and taking the extra time to understand the context within which it was written.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14129658419385619031" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1517676513" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1517676513" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1895570-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484874117p2/1895570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1517676513?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/1895570-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=postmodern">postmodern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1517676513" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8626832980853962557">This book was recommended by film director John Waters. I expected something a bit out of it and was not disappointed. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/100439.Jane_Bowles' title='Jane Bowles' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jane Bowles</a> has been associated in my mind strictly with her husband, fellow writer and beard Paul Bowles. In <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/215262.Two_Serious_Ladies_' title='Two Serious Ladies  by Jane Bowles' rel='nofollow noopener'>Two Serious Ladies</a>, Jane has anticipated the work of Argentinian writer César Aira in creating a work that drifts from event to event seemingly without any plan.<br/><br/>In an introduction to her work, Joy Williams wrote:<blockquote>There was no discernible narrative strategy. There was no w</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText8626832980853962557" style="display:none">This book was recommended by film director John Waters. I expected something a bit out of it and was not disappointed. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/100439.Jane_Bowles" title="Jane Bowles" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Bowles</a> has been associated in my mind strictly with her husband, fellow writer and beard Paul Bowles. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/215262.Two_Serious_Ladies_" title="Two Serious Ladies  by Jane Bowles" rel="nofollow noopener">Two Serious Ladies</a>, Jane has anticipated the work of Argentinian writer César Aira in creating a work that drifts from event to event seemingly without any plan.<br /><br />In an introduction to her work, Joy Williams wrote:<blockquote>There was no discernible narrative strategy. There was no way of explaining or analyzing the processes at work. Interpretation was useless. The vistas were dispiriting, the food foul, the wind always howling. Her people were mournful, impulsive, and as erratic in their particular journeys' flights as bats.</blockquote>The two serious ladies of the title are Christine Goering and Mrs. Copperfield. At first, we have a section on Miss Goering, in which she meets Mrs. Copperfield. Then we follow Mrs. Copperfield on a madcap voyage to Panama. The Final chapter brings the two together, but alas, they find they no longer have anything in common.<br /><br />Typical is this exchange in Section Three:<blockquote>"I don't know why you find it so interesting and intellectual to seek out a new city," said Arnold, cupping his chin in his hand and looking at her fixedly.<br /><br />"Because I believe the hardest thing for me to do is really move from one thing to another, partly," said Miss Goering.<br /><br />"Spiritually," said Arnold, trying to speak in a more sociable tone, "spiritually I'm constantly making little journeys and changing my entire nature every six months."<br /><br />"I don't believe it for a minute," said Miss Goering.<br /><br />"No, no, it is true. Also I can tell you that I think it is absolute nonsense to move physically from one place to another. All places are more or less alike."</blockquote>Curiously, it is Miss Goering who does most of the moving, while Arnold comes across as a couch potato.<br /><br />My only problem with <b>Two Serious Ladies</b> is that, without any real organization, the book could have gone on forever and stopped at any point. César Aira realizes this in his own books, which are always short and crisp, like a Roomba vacuum cleaner gone mad. Still, I find the book interesting, but tending to drag at the end.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8626832980853962557" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_998018292" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/998018292" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14941179-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353981100p2/14941179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/998018292?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/14941179-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/12394771-two-serious-ladies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=american-lit">american-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer998018292" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3254950963642137055">This novel is a delightfully deadpan examination of female friendships and how tedious it can be when men don’t listen. The protagonists are Miss Goering and Mrs Copperfield, both of whom are unsatisfied with their dull lives and therefore move to new places and consort with eccentric personages. I found both of them wonderfully honest, unaffected, and unconventional, which consistently confuses other characters, especially men. In my favourite scene, Miss Goering tries to start a conversation w</span>
  <span id="freeText3254950963642137055" style="display:none">This novel is a delightfully deadpan examination of female friendships and how tedious it can be when men don’t listen. The protagonists are Miss Goering and Mrs Copperfield, both of whom are unsatisfied with their dull lives and therefore move to new places and consort with eccentric personages. I found both of them wonderfully honest, unaffected, and unconventional, which consistently confuses other characters, especially men. In my favourite scene, Miss Goering tries to start a conversation with a stranger on a train and is severely reprimanded. I can imagine that happening in the UK, where the implicit social contract on trains remains ‘no eye contact, no starting conversations’.<br /><br />I found out about this novel when a friend linked me to this intriguing piece about Jane Bowles: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.newyorker.com/books/page-turner/the-madness-of-queen-jane">http://www.newyorker.com/books/page-t...</a> I really enjoyed Bowles’ writing style, which has a fresh and elegant quality about it. This novel is also wonderfully funny, seeming to parody literary melodrama and puncture pomposity. For example:<br /><br /><blockquote>”I’ll get down on my knees,” said Andy, shaking his fist at her. No sooner had he said this than he was down on his knees near her feet. The waiter was terribly shocked and felt that he had better say something.<br />“Look, Andy,” he said in a very small voice, “Why don’t you get up off your knees and think things over?”<br />“Because,” said Andy, raising his own voice more and more, “because she daren’t refuse a man who is down on his knees. She daren’t! It would be sacrilege.”<br />“I don’t see why,” said Miss Goering.<br />“If you refuse,” said Andy, “I’ll disgrace you, I’ll crawl out into the street, I’ll put you to shame.”<br />“I really have no sense of shame,” said Miss Goering.</blockquote><br /><br />I greatly enjoyed the characterisation of Miss Goering, Miss Gamelon, Mrs Copperfield, et al. The fact that women were referred to formally by their titles, whereas men generally only got a first name, also pleased me. After the misogyny of the last novel I read, this one made for a lovely change. The dialogue, which appears fraught with misunderstandings, is clever and would I think repay a re-read. What a pity that Bowles only completed this one novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3254950963642137055" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review998018292">

<div id="review_506009642" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/506009642" />
    <a title="David Corvine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12729742-david-corvine"><img alt="David Corvine" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/506009642?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Corvine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Corvine" href="/user/show/12729742-david-corvine">David Corvine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780956003850" href="/book/show/7662153-two-serious-ladies">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer506009642" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3894891969924731299">Two Serious Ladies: Jane Bowles.<br/><br/>	It is a great loss to literature that due to illness, both physical and psychological, that Two Serious Ladies represents the only full-length work produced by Jane Bowles. Only from this novel and a slim collection of short stories, " Everything is nice", in particular, can it be surmised what might have resulted from her long residence in Morocco and the relationships that she formed there. It can only be speculated as to what a mine and minefield such work mig</span>
  <span id="freeText3894891969924731299" style="display:none">Two Serious Ladies: Jane Bowles.<br /><br />	It is a great loss to literature that due to illness, both physical and psychological, that Two Serious Ladies represents the only full-length work produced by Jane Bowles. Only from this novel and a slim collection of short stories, " Everything is nice", in particular, can it be surmised what might have resulted from her long residence in Morocco and the relationships that she formed there. It can only be speculated as to what a mine and minefield such work might have been for critical theorists from a broad spectrum of disciplines.<br />	The characters in Two Serious Ladies inhabit a shadowy borderland between the probable and the possible... it is unlikely that an uninvited caller at an address, with only the most tenuous connection with the owner, should take up residence there the following day. Improbable but not impossible, Mrs. Bowles, in creating her strange and unnerving world never extends beyond the realms of reality. <br />	Both Mrs. Copperfield and Miss Goering seem to form and discontinue relationships in a decidedly arbitrary manner. They appear to be victims of hangers-on being buoyed along by the flow of the current. But careful analysis reveals that they are, in fact, making all the significant decisions. They are not unwitting passengers in the drunken boat they are at the helm.<br />	Bowles seems to create the impression that the ladies are in some way vulnerable, a sense of underlying threat pervades the text which erupts... breaks through the surface tension, in minor injuries, whether accidental or intentional. <br />	They don't lead normal lives but that is precisely because they are not normal. Ordinary people are burdened with the necessity of having to earn a living or are reliant on others to support them whereas the ladies are both financially independent. "After all, it's mostly my money," she (Mrs. Copperfield) said to herself. "I'm footing the bulk of the expenditure for this trip.". When Miss Goering perfunctorily decides to abandon her former home, with her erstwhile housemates still in residence. They make a request for financial assistance to find alternative accommodation. This elicits the ambivalent response... "No, no, that is not necessary. Is there anything more that is new?". Necessary? It certainly seems more necessary for the abandoned Miss Gamelon but Miss Goering has already moved off on another tangential shift and agreed to take up with a man who has mistaken her for a prostitute. He in turn discards her with as a little consideration as she herself seems to show to others.<br />	Unfortunately, Jane Bowles has been as underrated as she has been overlooked. The world she portrays with its random connections and the changes of direction is strangely portentous of our own age where people meet total strangers on the internet and arbitrarily abandon their settled lives to pursue them. Although this strangeness should not come as too much of a surprise... originality is, by its nature, strange.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3894891969924731299" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3476715243" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3476715243" />
    <a title="Robert Blumenthal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10110896-robert-blumenthal"><img alt="Robert Blumenthal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339293952p2/10110896.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3476715243?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Blumenthal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Blumenthal" href="/user/show/10110896-robert-blumenthal">Robert Blumenthal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3476715243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8394449306113635865">Yes, this is a weird little novel (just 200 pages). It is a novel that I would suspect influenced (or would have influenced) such modern day authors such as Rachel Cusk or Ottessa Moshfegh. The two "serious" ladies are Christina Goering and Mrs. Copperfield. both upper middle class ladies living in New York. They both, in their own way, leave their sheltered lives and embark on journeys that take them into the more seedy side of life. The first chapter deals with Mrs. Goering as she grows up a s</span>
  <span id="freeText8394449306113635865" style="display:none">Yes, this is a weird little novel (just 200 pages). It is a novel that I would suspect influenced (or would have influenced) such modern day authors such as Rachel Cusk or Ottessa Moshfegh. The two "serious" ladies are Christina Goering and Mrs. Copperfield. both upper middle class ladies living in New York. They both, in their own way, leave their sheltered lives and embark on journeys that take them into the more seedy side of life. The first chapter deals with Mrs. Goering as she grows up a strange little religious girl, then joins up with a woman and a man to live on an island near New York City. There is a very short encounter between her and Mrs. Copperfield. Then, chapter two shifts to Mrs. Copperfield, where she accompanies her husband on a vacation to Panama. Instead of doing the usual vacation activities with her handsome and companionable husband, she chooses to spend her time staying with local prostitutes. The third chapter shifts back to Mrs. Goering, and she is taking ferry trips to a local city across from her island, and she encounters some men of questionable repute.<br /><br />All this is told in a very modernist, somewhat scattered style. All of the characters are rather strange and were very hard for me to decipher. I'm sure there was a lot of satire going on, and the loneliness of the upper middle class woman was certainly addressed. It seemed very difficult for people to truly connect in this novel, and it was often very difficult for me to figure out how they were really feeling. And although I really enjoyed parts of it, I wasn't inspired enough to put it all together and be able to draw any real compassion for these characters. There is little real plot, and though I found some humor in it, certainly not enough to satisfy me. Overall, pretty much a disappointment for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8394449306113635865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1332919082" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1332919082" />
    <a title="Pascale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17430783-pascale"><img alt="Pascale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363794428p2/17430783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1332919082?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pascale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pascale" href="/user/show/17430783-pascale">Pascale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1332919082" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15502988755659624381">One of the most inconsequential books ever. I can't imagine why it got such endorsements from famous people. They must have been fond of the author or something. There is virtually no connection between Mrs Copperfiled and Christina Goering, except that initially they belong to the same New York social set. Then Mrs Copperfiled reluctantly follows her husband on a trip to South America, but whereas he is adventurous, she panics all the time and quickly decides to stay put in Panama with a new gr</span>
  <span id="freeText15502988755659624381" style="display:none">One of the most inconsequential books ever. I can't imagine why it got such endorsements from famous people. They must have been fond of the author or something. There is virtually no connection between Mrs Copperfiled and Christina Goering, except that initially they belong to the same New York social set. Then Mrs Copperfiled reluctantly follows her husband on a trip to South America, but whereas he is adventurous, she panics all the time and quickly decides to stay put in Panama with a new group of friends, namely Pacifica, a young prostitute, and Mrs. Quill who owns the hotel where Pacifica lives and turns tricks. Miss Goering, on the other hand, voluntarily gives up her mansion to relocate on a dismal property on Staten Island with another motley crew of eccentric but decent hangers-on. Eventually she abandons them to take up first with Andy, then briefly with Ben. This she seems to do as a sort of penance, because in fact she doesn't enjoy their company one bit. However, it is preposterous to claim, as Francine du Plessis Gray does in the introduction to my edition (Virago) that she turns into "a high class call-girl". Did we even read the same book? There is no evidence that Christina accepts money from either fellow, and clearly she has no interest in either sex or money. In fact both "heroines" can afford to do pretty much what they like, precisely because they have money, and to me they are having a lark rather than pursuing some kind of feminist path to empowerment. In spite of the odd insight here and there, this book has none of the wit and charm that I expected. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15502988755659624381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2810272029" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2810272029" />
    <a title="Lena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/220791-lena"><img alt="Lena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589815535p2/220791.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2810272029?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lena" href="/user/show/220791-lena">Lena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/220791-lena?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2810272029" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12683550191187683609">First published in 1943, this avant-garde novel tells the story of two well-to-do women who are compelled to shake up their lives and push themselves out of their comfort zones. Christina Goering sells her fancy house and moves to a comfortless cabin on an island with two unpleasant companions, while Frieda Copperfield abandons her husband in Panama and befriends a teenage prostitute. <br/><br/>It is a very odd book, about very odd characters, whose motivations throughout remained for the most part inscru</span>
  <span id="freeText12683550191187683609" style="display:none">First published in 1943, this avant-garde novel tells the story of two well-to-do women who are compelled to shake up their lives and push themselves out of their comfort zones. Christina Goering sells her fancy house and moves to a comfortless cabin on an island with two unpleasant companions, while Frieda Copperfield abandons her husband in Panama and befriends a teenage prostitute. <br /><br />It is a very odd book, about very odd characters, whose motivations throughout remained for the most part inscrutable to me. I imagine it would have made more sense to those contemporary with the book, or those with enough familiarity with its literary context to understand why it was so groundbreaking during its time.<br /><br />That said, I kept reading because there is something compelling about the writing. Bowles only novel contains some perfectly crafted bursts of dry wit, and while I did not really understand what drove these characters, I did want to find out where they ended up.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12683550191187683609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_307754647" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/307754647" />
    <a title="Eugene" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76519-eugene"><img alt="Eugene" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442490147p2/76519.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/307754647?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eugene" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eugene" href="/user/show/76519-eugene">Eugene</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer307754647" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3785595212213181808">a lightning strike, a revelation. populated by persons afflicted -- the two serious ladies of its title most so -- by some hilarious strain of nutty. each too acquiring a certain kind of self-proclaimed but not entirely inaccurate sainthood. "saint" a title to use advisedly, but there is something of the seeker and holy fool about these characters. an air of privilege perfumes our ladies but their disavowal of it through the casual violation or even destruction of propriety makes it seem the tra</span>
  <span id="freeText3785595212213181808" style="display:none">a lightning strike, a revelation. populated by persons afflicted -- the two serious ladies of its title most so -- by some hilarious strain of nutty. each too acquiring a certain kind of self-proclaimed but not entirely inaccurate sainthood. "saint" a title to use advisedly, but there is something of the seeker and holy fool about these characters. an air of privilege perfumes our ladies but their disavowal of it through the casual violation or even destruction of propriety makes it seem the transgressions and non sequiturs are actually the fastidious following of a much higher order. my edition has a great intro by lorna sage who reveals parenthetically that Christina Goering was "named after Jesus Christ and Hitler's aviation minister"(!) ...published in 1943 TWO SERIOUS LADIES can be thought of as a proto-beat novel -- only in the sense that it too seems a response and protesting statement to the bourgeois strictures from which it arises -- but otherwise a total sui generis. it's madcap, movingly in touch with despair, structurally profound, and in the best sense foolishly holy. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br />(neither here nor there but for some reason while reading it i was frequently reminded of <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=XTqw9R-W3Ak" rel="nofollow noopener">dead pan philosophy professor ray johnson</a>.)<br /><br /><a href="http://www.paulbowles.org/janeobituary.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">janes bowles' nytimes obit</a><br /><br />great article by <a href="http://books.google.com/books?id=o2IEAAAAMBAJ&amp;pg=PA70&amp;dq=jane+bowles+death&amp;lr=&amp;as_drrb_is=q&amp;as_minm_is=0&amp;as_miny_is=&amp;as_maxm_is=0&amp;as_maxy_is=&amp;as_brr=3&amp;cd=5#v=onepage&amp;q=jane%20bowles%20death&amp;f=false" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">stacey d'erasmo on jane bowles in OUT</a> which has this bit:<br /><blockquote><br />"It's possible," <a href="http://youtu.be/ioH-iQo-y9c" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Koestenbaum</a> tells <a href="http://www.staceyderasmo.com/index.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">me</a>, "that I worship Jane Bowles a little less than I did five or ten years ago. Self destructiveness isn't as easily idealized as you get older." It's true. The loneliness of Bowles that seemed grand to me at 20 now seems like a question that was never answered.<br /></blockquote><br /><a href="http://www.worldcat.org/search?qt=worldcat_org_all&amp;q=two+serious+ladies+jane+bowles" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">find it at the library</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3785595212213181808" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_960693724" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/960693724" />
    <a title="Amy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1396335-amy"><img alt="Amy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442451149p2/1396335.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/960693724?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy" href="/user/show/1396335-amy">Amy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780062283122" href="/book/show/18085520-two-serious-ladies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1396335-amy?shelf=boozy-reviews">boozy-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer960693724" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6074616051779237612">This is a deeply weird book that I adored and will probably pick up and read a few more times over the years. Jane Bowles (wife of Paul) produced only this novel, a play, and some short stories and letters, but she has a cult following that apparently included Tennessee Williams, who called this his favorite book.<br/><br/>It just so happens that there's a passage about gin that will give you a taste of her weirdly wonderful style:<br/><br/>"Now for a little spot of gin to chase my troubles away. There just isn't a</span>
  <span id="freeText6074616051779237612" style="display:none">This is a deeply weird book that I adored and will probably pick up and read a few more times over the years. Jane Bowles (wife of Paul) produced only this novel, a play, and some short stories and letters, but she has a cult following that apparently included Tennessee Williams, who called this his favorite book.<br /><br />It just so happens that there's a passage about gin that will give you a taste of her weirdly wonderful style:<br /><br />"Now for a little spot of gin to chase my troubles away. There just isn't any other way that's as good. At a certain point gin takes everything off your hands and you flop around like a little baby. Tonight I want to be a little baby."<br /><br />Yeah. So that's Jane and her strange little novel. You obviously need a gin drink to sip with your two serious ladyfriends, but what? I'm going to suggest a strange little gin drink called pink gin that nobody drinks anymore, perhaps because it isn't very good. But I'm pretty sure it will take everything off your hands.<br /><br />(It's actually pretty good. Give it a try.)<br /><br />Note: Pink gin is usually served without ice, but I like it over ice. If you want to be a purist and leave out the ice, put both the glass and the gin in the freezer first to chill them.<br /><br />Pink Gin<br /><br />3 drops Angostura bitters<br /><br />2 oz gin (Plymouth if you can get it)<br /><br />Lemon peel for garnish<br /><br />Splash the bitters into a short rocks glass and swirl it around to coat the glass. Add gin and give it a little stir. Add ice if you're so inclined. Garnish with lemon peel. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6074616051779237612" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('njlWWW4QVKPQsE35nKBuDOYBUIg4KfykpZHjKpiJqf/O/XOq7hydoNG9CD6IycKxZ+3jNEJ+nNKiPHh0IaE4xA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Nobody will ever know who killed her. It wasn\'t me. Here is her dead body&quot;. Our narrator is deeply shaken; she has no idea what to make of this. She is new to area, having moved her from her longtime home after the death of her husband, and she knows very few people. And she\'s a little shaky even on best days. Her brooding about this note quickly grows into a full-blown obsession, and she begins to devote herself to exploring the possibilities of her conjectures about who this woman was and how she met her fate. Her suppositions begin to find echoes in the real world, and with mounting excitement and dread, the fog of mystery starts to form into a concrete and menacing shape. But as we follow her in her investigation, strange dissonances start to accrue, and our faith in her grip on reality weakens, until finally, just as she seems be facing some of the darkness in her own past with her late husband, we are forced to face the prospect that there is either a more innocent explanation for all this or a much more sinister one - one that strikes closer to home.\n\nA triumphant blend of horror, suspense, and pitch-black comedy, \'Death in Her Hands\' asks us to consider how the stories we tell ourselves both guide us closer to the truth and keep us at bay from it. Janey<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText457506009707006283\" style=\"display:none\">An unforgettably exuberant and potent novel by a writer at the height of her powersTwo auditors for the U.S. egg industry go rogue and conceive a plot to steal a million chickens in the middle of the night—an entire egg farm’s worth of animals. Janey and Cleveland—a spirited former runaway and the officious head of audits—assemble a precarious, quarrelsome team and descend on the farm on a dark spring evening. A series of catastrophes ensues.Deb Olin Unferth’s wildly inventive novel is a heist story of a very unusual sort. Swirling with a rich array of voices, Barn 8 takes readers into the minds of these renegades: a farmer’s daughter, a former director of undercover investigations, hundreds of activists, a forest ranger who suddenly comes upon forty thousand hens, and a security guard who is left on an empty farm for years. There are glimpses twenty thousand years into the future to see what chickens might evolve into on our contaminated planet. We hear what hens think happens when they die. In the end the cracked hearts of these indelible characters, their earnest efforts to heal themselves, and their radical actions will lead them to ruin or revelation.Funny, whimsical, philosophical, and heartbreaking, Barn 8 ultimately asks: What constitutes meaningful action in a world so in need of change? Unferth comes at this question with striking ingenuity, razor-sharp wit, and ferocious passion. Barn 8 is a rare comic-political drama, a tour de force for our time.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"457506009707006283\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n Not least, in the sequence of poems that reflect on the meanings of the Grenfell Tower fire, Roger Robinson finds ways to move beyond a just indignation to uncover the undertones of experience that bring us nearer to the human reality of that event.\n\nThe collection’s title points to the underlying philosophy expressed in these poems: that earthly joy is, or ought to be, just within, but is often just beyond our reach, denied by racism, misogyny, physical cruelty and those with the class power to deny others their share of worldly goods and pleasures. A Portable Paradise is not the emptiness of material accumulation, but joy in an openness to people, places, the sensual pleasures of food and the rewards to be had from the arts of word, sound and visual enticement – in short an “insatiable hunger” for life. The poems express a fierce anger against injustice, but also convey the irrepressible sense that Roger Robinson cannot help but love people for their humour, oddity and generosity of spirit.\n\nThese are finely crafted poems, that reveal Robinson’s capacity to tell involving stories and capture the essence of a character in a few words, to move the emotions with the force of verbal expression, and engage our thoughts, as in the sequence of poems that reflect on just what paradise might be.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/100439.Jane_Bowles">About Jane Bowles</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/100439.Jane_Bowles"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1188820906p3/100439.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/100439.Jane_Bowles">Jane Bowles
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
138
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2140661368352903667">Born Jane Sydney Auer, Jane Bowles's total body of work consists of one novel, one play, and six short stories. Yet John Ashbery said of her: "It is to be hoped that she will be recognized for what she is: one of the finest modern writers of fiction in any language." Tennessee Williams called her the most underrated writer of fiction in American literature. During her lifetime and since her death </span>
  <span id="freeText2140661368352903667" style="display:none">Born Jane Sydney Auer, Jane Bowles's total body of work consists of one novel, one play, and six short stories. Yet John Ashbery said of her: "It is to be hoped that she will be recognized for what she is: one of the finest modern writers of fiction in any language." Tennessee Williams called her the most underrated writer of fiction in American literature. During her lifetime and since her death in 1973, she has been considered a writer's writer, little known to the general public but with a loyal following of intensely devoted readers. <br /><br />She was born in New York City on February 22, 1917, the daughter of Sidney Auer and Claire Stajer Auer. Her childhood was spent in Woodmere, Long Island. On her father's death in 1930, Jane and her mother moved back to Manhattan. As an adolescent she developed tuberculosis of the knee. Her mother took her to a sanatorium in Leysin, Switzerland, where she was put in traction for many months. During this time she developed an intense love of literature and an equally intense series of obsessions and fears. Upon her return to New York she began to experiment with writing a novel and with sexual adventures with men and women, though primarily with women.<br /><br />In 1937 she met Paul Bowles, and in the following year they were married and set off for a honeymoon in Central America, which was to be, in part, the locale of her novel <i>Two Serious Ladies</i>. The Bowleses went on to Paris, where she started writing and at the same time visited lesbian bars. The marriage remained a sexual marriage for about a year and a half, but after that Jane and Paul lived separate sexual lives. After returning to New York in 1938, the Bowleses went on to Mexico, where Jane continued to work on her novel and also met Helvetia Perkins, who was to become her lover.<br /><br /><i>Two Serious Ladies</i> was published in 1943. The reviews were mostly uncomprehending. Soon, Paul, who had been involved in the editing of <i>Two Serious Ladies</i>, began to write short stories, which were immediately published with great distinction. Jane, having published a few short stories, began to work on a novel, but ran up against a serious writer's block.<br /><br />In 1947 Paul went to Morocco to work on The Sheltering Sky. Jane followed him there the following year. She continued to struggle to work, and published several short stories, including her masterpiece, "Camp Cataract," and began to work seriously on her play <i>In the Summer House</i>. In Tangier, where the Bowleses resided, Jane fell in love with a Moroccan peasant woman.<br /><br /><i>In the Summer House</i> was performed on Broadway in 1953 to mixed reviews. Jane returned to Tangier and continued to try to write a novel, but her attention was primarily devoted to her love affair with Cherifa, the Moroccan woman, to affairs with other women and also to a social life in which she did a considerable amount of drinking.<br /><br />In 1957 she suffered a serious stroke, which affected her sight and her capacity to imagine. Nevertheless, notebook after notebook attests to her still continuing struggle to try to write. Her condition worsened, and after hospitalizations in England, New York and Málaga, Spain, she was confined in the Clinica de Los Angeles in Málaga, where she died in 1973.<br /><br />Yet it should be noted that despite this tragic story, her personality captivated many people. She was brilliant and witty, always doing and saying the unexpected thing. She was in every way as surprising as her work, one moment mystical, the next moment hilariously funny.<br /><br />Copyright © 2003, by Millicent Dillon</span>
  <a data-text-id="2140661368352903667" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
